Unit-1 & 2
1. Define research 
2. What are the objectives of research 
3. Significance of the research , explain?
4. What are the difference types of research.
5. Define research design?
6. What the need of research design?
7. Explain the types of research design?
8. Steps in research design?
9. What are the criteria should have good research?
10. Features or characteristics of a good research design?
11. Difference scales or types of scales?
12. Why you do have to define research problem, what are the problems faced by research problem?
Unit-3

1. Discuss about the primary data collection method with an examples?
2. Differentiate between the primary and secondary data collection?
3. Discuss about the interview and observation method?
4. Explain the difference between questionnaire and schedules?
5. Describe the content in the construction of questionnaire?
6. What is the meaning of validity and discuss it's difference types?
7. What are the steps involved in the deciding the determined of optimals sample size?
8. Discussing sampling techniques and methods?

Unit-4
1. Explain the characteristics of hypothesis describe the procedure of hypothesis testing?
2. Differentiate between quantitative and qualitative data?
3. Explain the meaning of analysis variance and also briefly explain the techniques of ANOVA  one-way and two-way classification?
4. Define bivariance and  multivariance with its objectives analysis classification?
5. Define correlation, regression,MDS and conjoint analysis?
6. What is chi-square and explain its significance in statistical analysis?

Unit-5 
1. What do you mean by research report?
2. What are the difference types of research report?
3. Define foot note?
4. Need for executive summary.
5. Discuss the content of report writing in detail.
6. Define tone.
7. Illustrate with an example the following terms of an research. 1.Objectivity. and 2.Subjectivity
8. Discuss the guidance for the formation of an research report.

Unit 3
1. What is levarage
2. What is indifference point or level of EBIT

7. What is bonus share ,reason for issue bonus share
8. 2mark. What is share ,or stock split,or reverse split

Unit 4

4. Working capital strategy

7. What is inventory types
8. Need for monitering and controlling
9. Cash management models
10. Working capital finance

Unit 5
1.what is Indian financial system
2. Capital markets and features
3. Primary market
4. What is leasing, types and advantages and disadvantage
5. different between hire purchase and leasing
6. What is vendors capital
7. What is private equity

[04/10/24, 9:39:13 AM] Divyarasi Amcet 🔰: Corporate failures often arise from inadequate strategic management practices. Here’s a deeper look at why companies fail and how strategic management can help avoid these pitfalls:

### Reasons for Corporate Failures

1. **Lack of Clear Strategy**:
   - Organizations may operate without a well-defined strategic plan, leading to inconsistent decisions and misaligned resources.

2. **Failure to Adapt**:
   - Many firms struggle to adjust to market changes, technological advancements, or shifts in consumer behavior, resulting in loss of competitiveness.

3. **Poor Execution**:
   - A solid strategy is ineffective if not executed properly. This includes insufficient communication and lack of accountability.

4. **Inadequate Market Understanding**:
   - Failing to conduct thorough market analysis can result in misjudging customer needs, leading to product failures.

5. **Ignoring Stakeholder Interests**:
   - Neglecting the interests of key stakeholders (employees, customers, investors) can erode trust and engagement.

6. **Overextension and Poor Resource Management**:
   - Rapid expansion without proper planning can stretch resources thin and lead to operational inefficiencies.

7. **Cultural Resistance to Change**:
   - A rigid corporate culture can stifle innovation and adaptability, making it difficult to respond to new challenges.

### How Strategic Management Can Prevent Failures

1. **Develop a Clear Vision and Mission**:
   - A well-articulated vision and mission guide decision-making and align the organization’s efforts toward common goals.

2. **Conduct Regular Environmental Scans**:
   - Use tools like SWOT and PESTEL analyses to continually assess the internal and external environment, identifying opportunities and threats.

3. **Set SMART Goals**:
   - Establish specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-bound objectives to provide clear targets for performance and growth.

4. **Foster a Culture of Innovation**:
   - Encourage creative thinking and experimentation, allowing the organization to adapt to market changes and seize new opportunities.

5. **Invest in Leadership Development**:
   - Cultivate strong leadership skills across the organization to drive strategy execution and inspire teams.

6. **Enhance Communication and Collaboration**:
   - Ensure effective communication of the strategic plan across all levels to align efforts and foster accountability.

7. **Monitor Performance Metrics**:
   - Regularly track key performance indicators (KPIs) to assess progress toward strategic goals and make necessary adjustments.

8. **Engage Stakeholders**:
   - Actively involve stakeholders in the strategic planning process to build trust and ensure their needs are considered.

9. **Implement Risk Management Frameworks**:
   - Develop comprehensive risk management strategies to identify, evaluate, and mitigate potential risks proactively.

10. **Encourage Continuous Learning**:
    - Promote a learning culture that embraces feedback and adapts based on lessons learned from both successes and failures.

### Conclusion

By focusing on these strategic management practices, organizations can enhance their resilience and adaptability, thereby reducing the risk of failure. Effective strategic management not only provides a roadmap for success but also prepares firms to navigate uncertainties and challenges in a dynamic business environment.

[15/11/24, 9:55:21 AM] Geetha Amcet🔰: In Strategic Human Resource Management (SHRM), values play a crucial role in shaping organizational culture, guiding employee behavior, and aligning HR practices with the long-term goals of the organization. Values can be classified in several ways depending on the context, but a common classification includes the following categories:

### 1. **Core Organizational Values**:
These are the fundamental principles that guide the behavior, decisions, and practices within an organization. They typically reflect the company's mission, vision, and strategic goals. Examples include:
   - Integrity
   - Innovation
   - Customer Focus
   - Accountability
   - Respect for diversity

### 2. **Ethical Values**:
Ethical values govern the moral framework within which the organization operates. They influence decision-making, fairness, and equity in HR practices. Common ethical values include:
   - Fairness and equity
   - Transparency
   - Ethical behavior
   - Respect for employee rights
   - Social responsibility

### 3. **Cultural Values**:
These values reflect the shared beliefs and behaviors that define how employees interact with each other and the organization as a whole. Cultural values can shape the workplace environment and influence the success of HR strategies in fostering teamwork, leadership, and employee satisfaction. Examples include:
   - Collaboration
   - Open communication
   - Continuous learning and development
   - Work-life balance
   - Trust

### 4. **Operational Values**:
These values are tied to the organization's day-to-day operations and the practical application of HR strategies. Operational values are crucial in translating strategic goals into HR practices. Examples include:
   - Efficiency
   - Innovation
   - Quality
   - Productivity

### 5. **Human Capital Values**:
These values are centered on the recognition, development, and utilization of the workforce as a critical asset to the organization's success. They highlight the importance of talent management and employee development in SHRM. Examples include:
   - Employee development
   - Talent recognition
   - Empowerment and autonomy
   - Lifelong learning
   - Career growth opportunities

### 6. **Strategic Alignment Values**:
These values ensure that HR policies and practices are aligned with the overall strategic objectives of the organization. Strategic alignment fosters a coherent approach to HR management that supports the organization's long-term goals. Examples include:
   - Agility and flexibility
   - Competitive advantage
   - Long-term sustainability
   - Organizational alignment with business strategy

By identifying and emphasizing these different types of values, strategic human resource management helps to ensure that the HR function contributes effectively to achieving organizational goals and enhancing overall performance.

[18/11/24, 9:34:52 AM] Geetha Amcet🔰: Corporate failures often arise from inadequate strategic management practices. Here’s a deeper look at why companies fail and how strategic management can help avoid these pitfalls:

### Reasons for Corporate Failures

1. **Lack of Clear Strategy**:
   - Organizations may operate without a well-defined strategic plan, leading to inconsistent decisions and misaligned resources.

2. **Failure to Adapt**:
   - Many firms struggle to adjust to market changes, technological advancements, or shifts in consumer behavior, resulting in loss of competitiveness.

3. **Poor Execution**:
   - A solid strategy is ineffective if not executed properly. This includes insufficient communication and lack of accountability.

4. **Inadequate Market Understanding**:
   - Failing to conduct thorough market analysis can result in misjudging customer needs, leading to product failures.

5. **Ignoring Stakeholder Interests**:
   - Neglecting the interests of key stakeholders (employees, customers, investors) can erode trust and engagement.

6. **Overextension and Poor Resource Management**:
   - Rapid expansion without proper planning can stretch resources thin and lead to operational inefficiencies.

7. **Cultural Resistance to Change**:
   - A rigid corporate culture can stifle innovation and adaptability, making it difficult to respond to new challenges.

### How Strategic Management Can Prevent Failures

1. **Develop a Clear Vision and Mission**:
   - A well-articulated vision and mission guide decision-making and align the organization’s efforts toward common goals.

2. **Conduct Regular Environmental Scans**:
   - Use tools like SWOT and PESTEL analyses to continually assess the internal and external environment, identifying opportunities and threats.

3. **Set SMART Goals**:
   - Establish specific, measurable, achievable, relevant, and time-bound objectives to provide clear targets for performance and growth.

4. **Foster a Culture of Innovation**:
   - Encourage creative thinking and experimentation, allowing the organization to adapt to market changes and seize new opportunities.

5. **Invest in Leadership Development**:
   - Cultivate strong leadership skills across the organization to drive strategy execution and inspire teams.

6. **Enhance Communication and Collaboration**:
   - Ensure effective communication of the strategic plan across all levels to align efforts and foster accountability.

7. **Monitor Performance Metrics**:
   - Regularly track key performance indicators (KPIs) to assess progress toward strategic goals and make necessary adjustments.

8. **Engage Stakeholders**:
   - Actively involve stakeholders in the strategic planning process to build trust and ensure their needs are considered.

9. **Implement Risk Management Frameworks**:
   - Develop comprehensive risk management strategies to identify, evaluate, and mitigate potential risks proactively.

10. **Encourage Continuous Learning**:
    - Promote a learning culture that embraces feedback and adapts based on lessons learned from both successes and failures.

### Conclusion

By focusing on these strategic management practices, organizations can enhance their resilience and adaptability, thereby reducing the risk of failure. Effective strategic management not only provides a roadmap for success but also prepares firms to navigate uncertainties and challenges in a dynamic business environment.

[18/11/24, 12:11:04 PM] Koms Amcet 🔰: Communication and Active Listening: Often, conflicts arise from misunderstandings. Open, honest, and respectful communication is key to resolving conflicts. Listening carefully to the other person's perspective can help both parties understand each other’s point of view.Collaboration: Involves working together to find a solution that satisfies both parties. This often requires creativity and compromise, where both sides may have to give a little to reach a mutually acceptable outcome.Mediation: Involves bringing in a neutral third party to facilitate the discussion and help the parties find common ground. The mediator doesn’t make decisions but helps guide the conversation and suggest solutions.Compromise: This is when both parties give up something to reach an agreement. While not all interests may be fully satisfied, the goal is to find a middle ground where both sides can live with the outcome.Negotiation: A more formal process where the parties involved discuss their differences and try to reach a mutually beneficial agreement. This often involves proposals, counter-proposals, and sometimes, concessions.Restorative Justice: Used in situations involving harm, where the focus is on repairing relationships and restoring harmony. This may involve acknowledging wrongdoing, making amends, and working toward healing.Problem-solving: Focuses on finding a practical solution to the issue at hand. This approach involves defining the problem, exploring potential solutions, and agreeing on an action plan to resolve it.

[06/12/24, 11:57:19 AM] Divya Amcet 🔰: The socio-economic status of Indian construction and agricultural laborers is characterized by a combination of low income, poor living conditions, and limited access to social services, although these can vary by region and specific labor categories. Here's an overview of their socio-economic status:

### 1. **Income Levels**:
   - **Construction Workers**: The income of construction laborers is often low and irregular, depending on the type of work, location, and seasonality. Workers typically earn daily wages, which are often insufficient to meet basic needs. Migrant workers, who make up a significant portion of the construction workforce, face additional challenges in terms of income stability.
   - **Agricultural Laborers**: Agricultural laborers, particularly those working in rural areas, typically earn low wages. Many are paid on a daily or piece-rate basis (e.g., per quantity of work done), which makes their income highly volatile. They are often employed in seasonal work such as harvesting or sowing.

### 2. **Living Conditions**:
   - **Construction Workers**: They usually live in poor conditions, often in temporary shelters near construction sites, which lack basic facilities like clean water, sanitation, and adequate healthcare. In many cases, they live in overcrowded spaces with little privacy.
   - **Agricultural Laborers**: Agricultural workers, especially those who are landless or working on others' farms, often live in simple, poorly constructed homes with limited access to essential services. The availability of sanitation, clean water, and electricity can be quite inadequate.

### 3. **Health and Education**:
   - **Construction Workers**: Health issues are prevalent among construction workers due to the physically demanding nature of the job, exposure to hazardous materials, and lack of access to healthcare. These workers often lack health insurance and social security benefits. Educational attainment is generally low, as most workers come from rural, marginalized backgrounds and are often illiterate or semi-literate.
   - **Agricultural Laborers**: Health problems, including malnutrition, exposure to pesticides, and lack of healthcare services, are common. The educational status of agricultural workers is similarly low, with limited access to formal education, especially for women and children in these households.

### 4. **Social Security and Benefits**:
   - **Construction Workers**: Although there are schemes like the *Building and Other Construction Workers Welfare Cess Act* that aim to provide benefits such as health and education, these are often poorly implemented, and workers rarely benefit from them. The informal nature of their employment means they are excluded from many social security programs.
   - **Agricultural Laborers**: Many agricultural workers do not have formal contracts and are excluded from government schemes. Those who work on a daily wage basis or in informal employment lack access to social security, including pensions, insurance, and maternity benefits.

### 5. **Migration**:
   - **Construction Workers**: A large number of construction laborers migrate from rural to urban areas in search of work, often resulting in a transient lifestyle. These workers often face exploitation by middlemen or contractors and lack any long-term job security or social support networks.
   - **Agricultural Laborers**: Migration among agricultural workers is often seasonal. They may move from areas of low agricultural productivity to regions where there are higher demands for labor during the harvest season. Seasonal migration leads to disruption in family life and social structures, contributing to their socio-economic vulnerability.

### 6. **Gender Inequality**:
   - **Construction Workers**: While both men and women work in construction, women often face discrimination, lower wages, and poor working conditions. They are also more vulnerable to exploitation and abuse.
   - **Agricultural Laborers**: Women in agriculture, especially in rural India, face additional challenges, including lower wages than men for similar work. They also have fewer opportunities for upward mobility and are typically engaged in the most labor-intensive and lower-paid tasks.

### 7. **Social Status**:
   - Both construction and agricultural laborers belong to the economically weaker sections of society, and many belong to marginalized communities, including Scheduled Castes (SCs), Scheduled Tribes (STs), and Other Backward Classes (OBCs). These groups often face social discrimination, which compounds their economic challenges.

### 8. **Government Schemes**:
   - Various government programs, such as the Mahatma Gandhi National Rural Employment Guarantee Act (MGNREGA), aim to improve the situation of agricultural and construction laborers by providing employment opportunities and wages. However, the effectiveness of these schemes is often undermined by issues such as corruption, lack of awareness, and inadequate implementation.

### Conclusion:
The socio-economic status of construction and agricultural laborers in India remains precarious. Their income is low and unstable, and they face numerous challenges related to living conditions, health, education, and social security. Despite some government initiatives aimed at improving their welfare, these workers remain among the most disadvantaged segments of Indian society, with limited access to resources and opportunities for upward mobility.

[11/12/24, 11:41:01 AM] Geetha Amcet🔰: Porter's Five Forces model is a business analysis tool that helps to assess the competitive forces within an industry, impacting its profitability. It includes five key forces:

1. **Threat of New Entrants**: This refers to the ease or difficulty with which new competitors can enter the industry. For the airline industry, the threat of new entrants is relatively low due to high barriers to entry, including significant capital investment in aircraft, regulatory approvals, airport slots, and established brand loyalty. However, low-cost carriers (LCCs) have been able to enter certain markets, challenging traditional airlines.

2. **Bargaining Power of Suppliers**: This force assesses the power of suppliers in driving up prices. In the airline industry, suppliers, particularly aircraft manufacturers (e.g., Boeing and Airbus), fuel suppliers, and airport operators, have significant power. Airlines are reliant on a small number of aircraft manufacturers, making them vulnerable to price hikes and supply chain disruptions. Fuel costs are volatile and a significant expense for airlines, further increasing supplier power.

3. **Bargaining Power of Buyers (Customers)**: This force looks at the power of customers in driving prices down. In the airline industry, customers have considerable bargaining power, especially with the rise of online travel agencies and comparison websites. Low-cost carriers also give customers more options. However, for premium services like business class or first class, the bargaining power is lower, as the target market is more niche and less price-sensitive.

4. **Threat of Substitute Products or Services**: This force examines the likelihood of customers switching to alternative products or services. In the airline industry, substitutes could include train travel, buses, or car rentals, especially for shorter distances. For long-haul flights, the threat of substitutes is lower. However, advancements in technology like virtual meetings can reduce the need for business travel, presenting a potential threat to airlines.

5. **Industry Rivalry**: This force refers to the level of competition within the industry. The airline industry is highly competitive, with numerous players competing on price, service, and routes. Rivalry is particularly intense in regions with multiple carriers, such as in the U.S. and Europe, where airlines engage in price wars and offer incentives like frequent flyer programs. Airline alliances and mergers have occurred to mitigate some of the competition, but overall, rivalry remains high.

### Application to the Airline Industry:
- **Barriers to Entry** are high due to capital requirements and regulatory hurdles, reducing the threat of new entrants, but low-cost carriers can still challenge established players in some markets.
- **Supplier Power** is substantial because there are few suppliers for key resources (aircraft, fuel), and fluctuations in fuel prices can have a significant impact on costs.
- **Buyer Power** is high as customers have many choices, especially with online booking platforms, and can easily compare prices.
- **Substitute Threat** is moderate, with alternative travel options like buses or trains available for short distances, but long-haul flights and business travel are less likely to be substituted.
- **Rivalry** is intense, especially in mature markets, where price wars and competitive offers dominate the industry.

The airline industry operates under strong competitive forces, with high rivalry, significant supplier power, and substantial buyer power. Airlines need to focus on differentiation, customer loyalty, and operational efficiency to maintain profitability.
